    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer341719073750558738">Insecure siblings fighting for their parents’ attention; bickering spouses who can’t stand to be together or apart; adultery and sexual experimentation; even the struggle to balance work and family: These are themes as much at home in our time as they were in the twelfth century. In James Goldman’s classic play <i>The Lion in Winter,</i> domestic turmoil rises to an art form. <br/>Kee</span>
  <span id="freeText341719073750558738" style="display:none">Insecure siblings fighting for their parents’ attention; bickering spouses who can’t stand to be together or apart; adultery and sexual experimentation; even the struggle to balance work and family: These are themes as much at home in our time as they were in the twelfth century. In James Goldman’s classic play <i>The Lion in Winter,</i> domestic turmoil rises to an art form. <br />Keenly self-aware and motivated as much by spite as by any sense of duty, Henry II and Eleanor of Aquitaine maneuver against each other to position their favorite son in line for succession. By imagining the inner lives of Henry, Eleanor, and their sons, John, Geoffrey, and Richard, Goldman created the quintessential drama of family strife and competing ambitions, a work that gives visceral, modern-day relevance to the intrigues of Angevin England. <br />Combining keen historical and psychological insight with delicious, mordant wit, the stage play has become a touchstone of today’s theater scene, and Goldman’s screenplay for the 1968 film adaptation won him an Academy Award. Told in “marvelously articulate language, with humor that bristles and burns” (<i>Los Angeles Times</i>), <i>The Lion in Winter</i> is the rare play that bursts into life on the printed page.</span>
  <a data-text-id="341719073750558738" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15228422166383087684"><b>THIS PLAY IS AN ABSOLUTE RIOT!</b> <p> It's Christmas time 1183 and the King of England is getting old....age 50....so it's time to decide if son Richard, Geoffrey or John will wear the Crown.</p><p> Siblings are <b>ALL</b> fighting for the title from oldest to youngest and King Henry II AND Queen Eleanor of Aquitaine, who have an on-going love-hate relationship, are in constant battle and disagreement.</p><p> <b>ALL</b> are liars goading one another, scheming to make the best deal. The Aquitaine is at stake so the lies, backstab</p></span>
  <span id="freeText15228422166383087684" style="display:none"><b>THIS PLAY IS AN ABSOLUTE RIOT!</b> <p> It's Christmas time 1183 and the King of England is getting old....age 50....so it's time to decide if son Richard, Geoffrey or John will wear the Crown.</p><p> Siblings are <b>ALL</b> fighting for the title from oldest to youngest and King Henry II AND Queen Eleanor of Aquitaine, who have an on-going love-hate relationship, are in constant battle and disagreement.</p><p> <b>ALL</b> are liars goading one another, scheming to make the best deal. The Aquitaine is at stake so the lies, backstabbing and unkept promises change by the minute, and to top things off, the young King of France is demanding the terms of a long ago made treaty be executed regarding a certain princess and her dowry....said vixen the current ward and lover of King Henry.</p><p> Such wicked humor and extreme family dysfunction....<b>ALL</b> vying for ultimate power made for a very entertaining read!</p><p>
  <b>UPDATE: August 11, 2019 - Oh the movie - Katharine Hepburn, Peter O'Toole, and an oh so young Anthony Hopkins deliver quite the performance as they "engage in a battle of Royal wits" in this 1968 classic, but that being said, even with the actors right in front of my eyes, I STILL prefer and enjoyed the written word more!</b>
</p></span>

<div id="review_2989803936" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2989803936" />
    <a title="Calista" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16731747-calista"><img alt="Calista" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1436384393p2/16731747.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2989803936?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Calista" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Calista" href="/user/show/16731747-calista">Calista</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16731747-calista?shelf=1960s">1960s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16731747-calista?shelf=bage-mature">bage-mature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16731747-calista?shelf=genre-play-theatre">genre-play-theatre</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16731747-calista?shelf=histiorical">histiorical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16731747-calista?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16731747-calista?shelf=want-to-own">want-to-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16731747-calista?shelf=genre-drama-tragedy">genre-drama-tragedy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16731747-calista?shelf=classic">classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2989803936" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15030584182263500200">Wow, what a political intrigue. This is not a sprawling as Game of Thrones, but it's similar to it. Who will get the throne of England when Henry dies. He and his wife hate/love each other and Henry has a lover in his bed now. Wife Eleanor is trying to put Richard I on the the throne and Henry wants to put John on the throne and no one wants Geoffrey. The time is 1188 and I assume this is historically based.<br/><br/>The characters were well rounded, devious, and everyone is willing to go to war or kill t</span>
  <span id="freeText15030584182263500200" style="display:none">Wow, what a political intrigue. This is not a sprawling as Game of Thrones, but it's similar to it. Who will get the throne of England when Henry dies. He and his wife hate/love each other and Henry has a lover in his bed now. Wife Eleanor is trying to put Richard I on the the throne and Henry wants to put John on the throne and no one wants Geoffrey. The time is 1188 and I assume this is historically based.<br /><br />The characters were well rounded, devious, and everyone is willing to go to war or kill to take the throne. The funny thing about the play is we never find out who took the throne. I am assuming it was Richard I, but it might have been John. <br /><br />These were some brutal times. King Henry wants to divorce Eleanor so he can marry his lover and then have more sons for the throne. He has disowned his sons and wants to lock them up. Though, if he leaves them in the dungeon and they get out, he knows they will kill the young new heirs. <br /><br />I have to say that it came as a surprise, but Richard was gay and having an affair with Phillip, King of France. I didn't see that coming and I do wonder if that is historically based. I know that stuff happens in history, but I have never heard that and I'm also surprised it was used in 1960s America. <br /><br />Of the the scheming characters, for some reason I have a fondness for Richard. I'm not sure why. I do feel for middle Geoffrey as he was so forgotten. The intrigue here is intense and this would be a horrible way to grow up or live in this toxic environment. <br /><br />My flute teacher recommended this play to me saying he loved it. I'm glad I finally read this and checked it off my list. These characters are humans, they have good sides and bad sides. They are willing to do horrible things for power. They are very complex. It's a wonderful play.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15030584182263500200" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_128370639" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/128370639" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4235780-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/128370639?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/4235780-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140481747" href="/book/show/762414.The_Lion_in_Winter">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4235780-chris?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4235780-chris?shelf=almost-poetry">almost-poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4235780-chris?shelf=anglophilia">anglophilia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4235780-chris?shelf=best-plays">best-plays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer128370639" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13482547442528236576"><i>
  <b>What shall we hang, the holly or each other?</b>
</i><br/><br/>If that sounds like something you'd hear (or say) at one of your holiday gatherings, then it's time for this Festivus classic. Wait, <i>Festivus</i> classic? Absolutely. While Die Hard may be an alternate Christmas classic for those who avoid the overly heartwarming by watching Bruce Willis <i>blow stuff up real good,</i> this is the movie (play, I meant play!*) for those who prefer their explosions verbal and emotional. <br/><br/>But why Festivus and not Christmas? This </span>
  <span id="freeText13482547442528236576" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>What shall we hang, the holly or each other?</b>
</i><br /><br />If that sounds like something you'd hear (or say) at one of your holiday gatherings, then it's time for this Festivus classic. Wait, <i>Festivus</i> classic? Absolutely. While Die Hard may be an alternate Christmas classic for those who avoid the overly heartwarming by watching Bruce Willis <i>blow stuff up real good,</i> this is the movie (play, I meant play!*) for those who prefer their explosions verbal and emotional. <br /><br />But why Festivus and not Christmas? This is, after all, set at Christmas. Henry II gathers together his family...the queen, Eleanor of Aquitaine, and his sons, Richard, Geoffrey, and John. Add to this the young King of France, Phillip, who has come to insist his sister Alais marry Richard, as was arranged over a decade earlier in exchange for giving a French province to England as dowry. <br /><br />So...it's a Christmas gathering, there's the occasional carol, presents are wrapped...why Festivus? Simply put, this <s>movie</s> play embodies everything that is awesome about Festivus! There are a few feats of strength, but really, this is all about the <i>airing of the grievances!</i> And the grievances are endless...Henry usually keeps Eleanor imprisoned in a castle, although to be fair, she has led civil wars against him. Henry and Eleanor vehemently disagree over who will succeed the throne on Henry's death,** their eldest son having died. Eleanor backs Richard; Henry sides with John. No one favours Geoffrey (who at one point sums up his position as, <i>"It's not the power I feel deprived of, it's the mention I miss"</i>). Henry also wants Alais to marry John, not Richard. Oh, and Alais happens to be Henry's mistress. Awkward! Henry and Phillip banter and barter back and forth, Henry embodying age and experience...<i>"By God, but I do love being king!</i>"... and Phillip striving to establish himself as someone to be reckoned with...<i>"I am a KING! I am no man's boy!"</i> And there's a scene with tapestries...<i>"That's what tapestries are for!"</i>...that I won't spoil. <br /><br />I cannot recommend this highly enough, as this volatile situation is delineated by Goldman's vicious, biting, sarcastic prose. I can guarantee you will find yourself quoting this play at some point, probably when your family gathers for Festivus!<br /><br /><b><s>HAPPY</s> BITTER FESTIVUS!</b><br /><br /><br /><br />* I really mean movie. I have both seen and read the play, but seriously, this review is really about the movie version with Peter O'Toole, Katherine Hepburn, Anthony Hopkins, and Timothy Dalton...it's one of those perfectly cast movies that everyone should see. <br /><br />** Both Richard <i>and</i> John succeeded Henry. Richard first, who went off on Crusades leaving Prince John in charge. Then John, who became king after Richard died. (Geoffery died and so was out of luck.) If these names sound familiar, yes they are the Richard and John in all the Robin Hood legends.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13482547442528236576" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12973395732827180200" style="display:none"><i>“We are the world in small. A nation is a human thing; it does what we do, for our reasons. Surely, if we’re civilized, it must be possible to put the knives away. We can make peace. We have it in our hands.”</i><br /><br />God, I love this play. Almost every line is golden.<br /><br /><i>“Well—what shall we hang? The holly or each other?”</i><br /><br />It’s Christmas, 1183, and King Henry II, Henry Plantagenet, is a force of nature, a whirlwind of intensity and macho. <br /><br />He’d thrown his queen, Eleanor of Aquitaine, in prison ten years previously, and has brought her back for Christmas Court. Their three sons, Richard, Geoffrey and John are each a very different piece of work, and their parents are sparring over which of their unfit children should inherit the throne.<br /><br />The 1968 film version starring the magnificent pairing of Peter O’Toole and Katharine Hepburn is a favorite of mine (a must see!), but this was my first reading. I’ve always thought the acting was brilliant, and like most plays, it’s better to see it performed. But what material they were given.<br /><br /><i>Henry: “Give me a little peace.” <br />Eleanor: “A little? Why so modest? How about eternal peace? Now there’s a thought.”</i></span>

<div id="review_953020621" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Marquise" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4674014-marquise"><img alt="Marquise" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445405166p2/4674014.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=best-loved-reads">best-loved-reads</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer953020621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12501702981183388589">I've just spent a happy Saturday finishing this delicious book, which did live up to expectations. <br/><br/>The Henry and Eleanor dynamics in this play would perhaps be best described as "a loving hate relationship," for you never are sure what exactly they feel for each other till the final scene, when you realise they are each other's weakness. The dialogue is wonderfully snarky and poisonously witty, like watching the two greatest swordsmen of the time duel for hours and hours non-stop, only that both</span>
  <span id="freeText12501702981183388589" style="display:none">I've just spent a happy Saturday finishing this delicious book, which did live up to expectations. <br /><br />The Henry and Eleanor dynamics in this play would perhaps be best described as "a loving hate relationship," for you never are sure what exactly they feel for each other till the final scene, when you realise they are each other's weakness. The dialogue is wonderfully snarky and poisonously witty, like watching the two greatest swordsmen of the time duel for hours and hours non-stop, only that both use words instead of weapons; both give as much as they take, both parry and wound and go down just to spring back to their feet, and when you think one of them has lost and will admit defeat out of sheer exhaustion... Forget that deceptive impression, they just unsheathe their claws again. And their boys are far from acting as squires or public: they happily step in and fight themselves a series of private duels. The only victim here, if she can be called so for she also has some pluck, is Alaïs, who at one point groans that there are "kings, queens, knights everywhere you look, and I'm the only pawn..."<br /><br />Those inveterate Angevins are at their best here, the book earns therefore a place amongst my keepers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12501702981183388589" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1019213865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6503968410186636394">Extraordinary. <br/><br/>I have a problem with despicable people. I have a bias against them, so shoot me. If you suck, I garner no enjoyment from your suckdom, and therefore will not typically read a book that is strictly about lying, conniving, murderous, adulterous, dare I say slimy?, people. Though we know little of their true personalities, I believe those adjectives could easily fit Henry II, his wife Eleanor and their crew, based on documented wars and murders alone. <br/><br/>But I did, enjoy it that is, an</span>
  <span id="freeText6503968410186636394" style="display:none">Extraordinary. <br /><br />I have a problem with despicable people. I have a bias against them, so shoot me. If you suck, I garner no enjoyment from your suckdom, and therefore will not typically read a book that is strictly about lying, conniving, murderous, adulterous, dare I say slimy?, people. Though we know little of their true personalities, I believe those adjectives could easily fit Henry II, his wife Eleanor and their crew, based on documented wars and murders alone. <br /><br />But I did, enjoy it that is, and the wonder of it all is that this play is written so well that these crazy people became (holy cheese, I'm gonna say it), <i>endearing</i>. <br /><br />On top of that, this play reads well on paper. So for anyone whose experience in reading plays hasn't ventured out beyond Shakespeare in high school, this would be a great next go. <br /><br />For those with some experience reading plays, I dare you not to take up your part in the mirror. The lines are so rich and biting and <i>funny</i> that you have to pause while reading to search for just the perfect inflection. Truly gorgeous. <br /><br />I've never seen the movie (either the 1968 Peter O'Toole/Katharine Hepburn nor the 2003 Glenn Close rendition), but I don't have too. The characters are already alive for me, thanks to James Goldman's extraordinary talent.<br /> <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6503968410186636394" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9468264306424225493">Was quite disappointed with this one, after the expectations had been set high. Here you just don't see living people behind the sophisticated and poignant exchanges that do not seem to take any toll on the participants, whereas in real l</span>
  <span id="freeText9468264306424225493" style="display:none">Was quite disappointed with this one, after the expectations had been set high. While this is a fine mind teaser and the dialogues are intellectually extremely intense, the play feels hollow. In this way, it's similar to the movie "Inception": an exciting premise that is not given a worthy incarnation in terms of character and story depth. Here you just don't see living people behind the sophisticated and poignant exchanges that do not seem to take any toll on the participants, whereas in real life it would have wreaked unimaginable havoc. Maybe the play makes a different impression on the stage, though.</span>

<div id="review_310795622" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/310795622" />
    <a title="jennifer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1345191-jennifer"><img alt="jennifer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1257560443p2/1345191.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/310795622?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="jennifer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="jennifer" href="/user/show/1345191-jennifer">jennifer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer310795622" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11652165998483371641">This is the play that the movie came from, and it's one of the most exciting and witty plays written. My edition has an interesting introduction by Goldman in which he relates how many people believe that the movie was made because the play was a big hit, which wasn't true. It was the movie, released over two years after the play had closed its brief Broadway run (with Christopher Walken as King Philip) that turned the play into a classic.<br/><br/>It's Christmas, 1183, and the three princes, Richard, Geo</span>
  <span id="freeText11652165998483371641" style="display:none">This is the play that the movie came from, and it's one of the most exciting and witty plays written. My edition has an interesting introduction by Goldman in which he relates how many people believe that the movie was made because the play was a big hit, which wasn't true. It was the movie, released over two years after the play had closed its brief Broadway run (with Christopher Walken as King Philip) that turned the play into a classic.<br /><br />It's Christmas, 1183, and the three princes, Richard, Geoffrey and John have gathered at their father's palace. Joining them from her exile is their mother, Queen Eleanor, who Henry has kept under guard somewhere for ten years while he took their French adopted daughter, Princess Alais, as his lover. On this day, Alais' brother, the king of France, has come to demand either the return of his sister or the fulfillment of Henry's contract: that Alais marry Richard and inherit the Aquitaine. That Henry does not want to give up Alais, his property or be ordered about are the immediate problems, but the bigger problem, and the plot of the play, is that Henry and Eleanor were and are horrible parents. Richard rages and openly desires to kill his father, Geoffrey repays his family for neglecting him by setting them up to be caught in his lies and John throws tantrums to guilt his father into giving him the crown. The dialogue is sharp, brutal and funny in a "see, your family could be worse" way that I love. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11652165998483371641" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2812496506" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14928311445748460625">Swift, engaging read. Goldman's intro is a gem that covers ground beyond this play. Here's a bit on what he has to say about writing the characters-- his 1980 perspective on this 1964 play:<br/><br/>I know that I have never met these characters. I made them up. I read about the things they did, I studied them and then imagined what they felt and thought and said and wanted from their lives. What they were really like, of course, no one will ever know. This is, I am convinced, a blessing, and I feel dismay</span>
  <span id="freeText14928311445748460625" style="display:none">Swift, engaging read. Goldman's intro is a gem that covers ground beyond this play. Here's a bit on what he has to say about writing the characters-- his 1980 perspective on this 1964 play:<br /><br />I know that I have never met these characters. I made them up. I read about the things they did, I studied them and then imagined what they felt and thought and said and wanted from their lives. What they were really like, of course, no one will ever know. This is, I am convinced, a blessing, and I feel dismay for all the people who, a thousand years from now, will have our times on tape and film to study. They will see out faces, hear our voices, know it all and be deceived. They will be dealing with the surfaces, and the truth of things is always underneath. It has to be imagined. <br /><br />The cover photo with Preston and Harris is also moving. No credit given to photographer and I imagine Martha Swope may have taken it-- has the intimacy of her work. How wonderful if the Preston-Harris production is preserved on film somewhere, however that record may deceive us.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3184956049" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3184956049" />
    <a title="Nicole-Rose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52457100-nicole-rose"><img alt="Nicole-Rose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580308574p2/52457100.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3184956049?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicole-Rose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicole-Rose" href="/user/show/52457100-nicole-rose">Nicole-Rose</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780573611605" href="/book/show/279663.The_Lion_in_Winter">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52457100-nicole-rose?shelf=oh-the-drama">oh-the-drama</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52457100-nicole-rose?shelf=angevin-shenans">angevin-shenans</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52457100-nicole-rose?shelf=plantagenet">plantagenet</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52457100-nicole-rose?shelf=richard-i">richard-i</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3184956049" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16686019901228890036"><b>Remarks:</b> Have you ever had a new book sit on your shelf for weeks without being read and you can't start it because you're waiting for that <i>perfect</i> moment to start reading it? Then after you finish it you wonder how stupid you were for letting it sit there for so long? Yeah, me too.  <br/><br/>Goldman's <i>The Lion in Winter</i> was just as good as the incredible hype surrounding it. As character-driven as this play was, I feel it would only be appropriate to go over some of its stand-out characters one-by-one. </span>
  <span id="freeText16686019901228890036" style="display:none"><b>Remarks:</b> Have you ever had a new book sit on your shelf for weeks without being read and you can't start it because you're waiting for that <i>perfect</i> moment to start reading it? Then after you finish it you wonder how stupid you were for letting it sit there for so long? Yeah, me too.  <br /><br />Goldman's <i>The Lion in Winter</i> was just as good as the incredible hype surrounding it. As character-driven as this play was, I feel it would only be appropriate to go over some of its stand-out characters one-by-one. Before that, I literally only have <i>one</i> gripe. Even after reading the author's note about his anachronistic depiction of Christmas, I still found it really distracting to picture the eaglets decorating a Christmas tree and Eleanor wrapping Christmas presents for her sons. I really don't think these elements were necessary. It may have been added for comedic effect, but I feel as if the play could have been even better without them. <br /><br />So let's get on with it. <br /><br /><i>"My life, when it is written, will read better than it lived."</i> - Henry<br /><br /><b>Henry:</b> Henry definitely had the most character in this story, though I feel as if we still didn't know too much about his true intentions by the end, which makes me think that his intentions were unknown even to him. As the play progresses, our understanding of what relationship remains between him and his wife gets clearer. I really would have loved to know how Henry really felt towards Eleanor during their elder years, but of course we'll never know. That being said, as Henry drops his guard, we see that there is still something between them. I feel like Goldman's Henry was the most believable in terms of what we know about the real Henry and I'm truly impressed with the depiction of him in such a short play. <br /><br /><i>"That's my one advantage over you. You're wiser, shrewder, more experienced. I'm colder; I feel less."</i> - Richard <br /><br /><b>Richard:</b> Ah Richard. Goldman's Richard is far from a soliloquist, but he definitely has the best one-liners in this play. So much so that I think the author could have removed all of his character tags from the script and I still would have known when it was him talking. It kind of threw me off that Richard was actually fighting <i>to</i> marry Alais since I'm pretty sure he was actually fighting with all of his being <i>not</i> to have to wed her, but I digress. The only aspect of his character that sort of surprised me is when he was extremely heartless and careless… until he wasn't. I really wasn't expecting him to get that emotionally wounded by Philip but I don't think it took anything away from his character. I think Goldman could have done something with that expression but it wasn't really touched upon after that. Maybe if it was a longer play.    <br /><br /><i>"If you're so clever than why am I always doing something stupid?"</i> - John<br /><br /><b>John:</b> Goldman's John is a child, and I love him that way. As hard as it is to admit, I've always liked over-the-top depictions of John. The brattier the better. To my delight, Goldman's John was an absolute asshat. Throughout the play, John battles with both his father and Richard, using Geoffrey as his henchman in the progress. Whereas Richard has a very hot and cold relationship with Eleanor, John's is just plain cold. John's a really enjoyable character to hate and I love the archetype of a character that feigns bravado yet can't seem to make a decision without being spurred on by another (see above quotation). <br /><br /><i>"I don't have to fight to win. Take all you want -- this country, that one. You won't keep it long."</i> - Philip<br /><br /><b>Philip:</b> OK, so. Philip is a very interesting historical character because he tends to be portrayed one of two ways; these two ways being <i>completely opposite</i> of each other. One common persona that is used for Philip is the pious and inexperienced child who will never be able to stand shoulder-to-shoulder with Henry. Goldman did not choose to use this Philip. At first I thought Goldman was going to be very illusive with this Philip's relationship with the sons, but he really doesn't leave us guessing for long. We really aren't sure in the end if Philip was lying about whether he callously used Richard or not. All we know is that, despite his young age, he seems to have Henry's entire brood in the palm of his hand. It's not exactly my preference, but I have no problem with this Philip, and I think Goldman did well with the decision he made to use him.<br /><br />That's it for now. This play was quite literally one of my most enjoyable reads ever, bias intended. I would literally fall out my chair if I had any opportunity to see this played out in the future. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16686019901228890036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Drew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Drew" href="/user/show/1752430-drew">Drew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1752430-drew?shelf=drama">drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2272886348" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17553175194139698659">A very good play but I wonder if I liked it so much since I saw the 1968 film with Hepburn & O’Toole. Without imagining them saying these lines, it would have been more dry & I’d likely have gone for a 3 Star rating. The dialogue is biting and witty, but partly due to delivery and the actors are the ones to add dimensionality to the lines. I recommended both reading this and watching that fantastic movie!</span>
  <span id="freeText17553175194139698659" style="display:none">A very good play but I wonder if I liked it so much since I saw the 1968 film with Hepburn &amp; O’Toole. Without imagining them saying these lines, it would have been more dry &amp; I’d likely have gone for a 3 Star rating. The dialogue is biting and witty, but partly due to delivery and the actors are the ones to add dimensionality to the lines. I recommended both reading this and watching that fantastic movie!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4141540397475465240">You really need to see this I think to keep up with the reversals. The voice characterizations are well done so that each person has a distinct voice, but there's a tiny bit of overlap with Philip and with John. A fast moving bit of betrayal, counter betrayal, play-acting, acting, seriousness, treachery, counter-treachery and it all moves along at such speed. I think seeing the characters would help keep the players a bit more distinct, but otherwise, this is a treat.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1204792695" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1204792695" />
    <a title="Susana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12047133-susana"><img alt="Susana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1346671784p2/12047133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1204792695?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susana" href="/user/show/12047133-susana">Susana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140481747" href="/book/show/762414.The_Lion_in_Winter">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12047133-susana?shelf=theatre">theatre</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12047133-susana?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1204792695" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4541231560320180649">The love of power, the power of love and the power of power. Lovely. Those are the three things the play is about according to Michael Mayer, one of its directors.<br/><br/>I've just watched two plays that have absolutely nothing to do with one another despite being pretty much about the same thing: a dysfunctional family get-together for a special occasion (Christmas in one, the grandfather's suicide in the other). The one is the Lion in Winter, the other August: Osage county.<br/><br/>It's Christmas 1183. Henry I</span>
  <span id="freeText4541231560320180649" style="display:none">The love of power, the power of love and the power of power. Lovely. Those are the three things the play is about according to Michael Mayer, one of its directors.<br><br>I've just watched two plays that have absolutely nothing to do with one another despite being pretty much about the same thing: a dysfunctional family get-together for a special occasion (Christmas in one, the grandfather's suicide in the other). The one is the Lion in Winter, the other August: Osage county.<br><br>It's Christmas 1183. Henry II has assembled his three ungrateful sons, Richard, Geoffrey and John, his estranged wife, Eleanor of Aquitaine, and Alais, his ward, lover and the fiancée of whichever might be his heir. Her brother Phillip tags along, as he's the king of France and Henry's suzerain. During the holidays, Henry aims to name a successor. I love history, things are always so messed up. <br><br>Alais was raised by the then loving couple and looks up to Eleanor as a mother. Eleanor is Phillip's father ex-wife.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1424340885ra/13749193.gif" width="150" height="150" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1424340885ra/13749194.gif" width="150" height="150" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Facts are stranger than fiction, guys.<br><br>However, the play is only apparently historical. Facts are but the byproduct of the relationships. And what a relationship is Eleanor and Henry's! A classical love/hate that translates into an epic and constant power struggle. He's "a marvel of a man" and she's every bit his match.<br><br>The earliest example of power struggle I remember is in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10791718.Rocambole__Colecci_n_Completa_" title="Rocambole (Colección Completa) by Pierre Alexis Ponson du Terrail" rel="nofollow noopener">Rocambole</a> in which a fallen woman and a criminal mastermind try to convince each other that they've nothing but trust and love for one another. She would love nothing more than shoot him in the face. He'd delight in seing her suffer and then die. At stake, their lives plus that of her loved ones.<br><br>And that defines a power struggle. Two characters walk into a room. Each wants something from the other. The question is, who gets what they want? <br><br>One of the best is Clarice Starling and Hannibal Lecter's first meeting. For a far better analysis than I could do, watch this video: <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=5V-k-p4wzxg" rel="nofollow noopener">The Silence of the Lambs - Who Wins the Scene?</a><br><br>Tarantino also loves this kind of scene. Some of his movies are nothing but a string of power struggles with violent releases. Inglorious Basterds comes to mind. His shifting power dynamics are somewhat less effective because there's simply too many of them. For it to be a really good one the characters involved must love the game and respect each other's abilities. Therein lies Hans Landa's scenes' success.<br><br>Henry and Eleanor are able to sustain this dynamic during the whole play. He left her for softer women who don't scheme and plot his death with every breath. Can you believe it? <br>She looks at him and sees "cities, acreage, coastline, taxes" too. He looks at her and sees the Aquitaine too. They flirt and hurt each other and never let go of their goals.<br><i><br>Henry II: Time hasn't done a thing but wrinkle you.<br>Eleanor: It hasn't even done that. I've borne six girls, five boys... and thirty-one connubial years of you. How am I possible?<br>Henry II: There are moments when I miss you.<br>Eleanor: Many?<br>Henry II: Do you doubt it? </i><br><br><i> Henry II: Don't fight me, Eleanor.<br>Eleanor: What would you have me do? Give out, give up, give in?<br>Henry II: Give me a little peace.<br>Eleanor: A little? Why so modest? How about eternal peace? Now, there's a thought. <br></i> <br>Their sons just can't live up to them. Richard depends on his mother to get the kingdom for him. To do this he employs the curious strategy of telling her he neither loves her nor trusts her. John relies on his father to do so but betrays him at the first chance he gets. Geoffrey promises and lies and nobody but John ever believes him.<br><br>Why isn't he ever considered to be king, he and I both wonder? There's really not much of a difference between his behaviour and his mother's. They both negotiate with nothing (the Aquitaine is Richard's). But Eleanor is going to win, as anybody familiar with Robin Hood knows. While it's dubious she truly loves Richard, she's humanized by her sincere love of Henry and Alais whereas Geoffrey is "a device. He's wheels and gears".<br><br>Eleanor is priceless. A powerful duchess of her own right, two times queen and once regent. She's thought to have been extroverted, intelligent, strong willed and cultivated. One of the biggest patrons of the arts of her age, she headed a Cour d'Amour in Poitiers with her daughter Marie de Champagne.<br><br>In the movie she's played by Katharine Hepburn. The cast is all-around awesome but she's in a league of her own. Which other actress, ever, could play such a smart, independent, fierce, dry, funny woman? My second choices would be Maggie Smith (because she's Maggie Smith) or Emma Thompson (because of Much Ado about Nothing). Maybe Meryl Streep (because she can do anything).<br><br>If we add a truly masterful use of tapestries we get one of the best plays I've ever have the pleasure of reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4541231560320180649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_306000030" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/306000030" />

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer306000030" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10967401049195030448">I'm about to start rehearsing to play Richard in this play, which goes up in June. It's a revisiting. I read it in college when I helped build the set for an OSU theater department production. Eight years later I played Geoffrey in a community theater version. Now on to Richard.<br/><br/>The play takes place on a Christmas Day in the twelfth century, and concerns a unique dysfuctional family who just happen to be the Royal Family of England. There's the father, Henry II, trying to effect a peaceful transi</span>
  <span id="freeText10967401049195030448" style="display:none">I'm about to start rehearsing to play Richard in this play, which goes up in June. It's a revisiting. I read it in college when I helped build the set for an OSU theater department production. Eight years later I played Geoffrey in a community theater version. Now on to Richard.<br /><br />The play takes place on a Christmas Day in the twelfth century, and concerns a unique dysfuctional family who just happen to be the Royal Family of England. There's the father, Henry II, trying to effect a peaceful transition of power to one of his sons while marrying his mistress, Alais. There's the mother, Eleanor of Aquitaine, the richest woman in the world, who is trying to finagle her freedom and the accession to power of another son. These two hate one another passionately, although the ghost of their love lingers.<br /><br />And then there are the boys. Richard, a bloody-minded warrior, Geoffrey, an intellectual plotter, and John, a sniveling brat. Add to the mix the young French king, Philip, and his sister, the above-mentioned Alais.<br /><br />Plots and schemes abound, as do witty putdowns, as the characters plot each other's deaths and simultaneously cry out for each other's love.<br /><br />Three key observations this time around:<br /><br />1) This is what the world would look like if psychiatry had never been invented. The family members practice their "talking cures" with one another, although no cure is in sight.<br /><br />2) This is the original "Game of Thrones", what with its combination of cutting, witty barbs and internecine plots.<br /><br />3) In many ways, the Plantagenet Family Christmas is the original "Festivus", complete with the airing of grievances, but without the pole and feats of strength. <br /><br />Doing this show again is going to be fun.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10967401049195030448" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('a3A7wCgn8AL96O9DVIY5lQ5sgS2sxUnj/uZAjfHn4fIYIRdx/2FYcaEUUBEDcjz2sGs0ZXRTLLM7ZGDH2fjyTQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<script>

Having recently demolished another car, Daisy Wertham, a rich, sharp-tongued Jewish widow of seventy-two, is informed by her son, Boolie, that henceforth she must rely on the services of a chauffeur. The person he hires for the job is a thoughtful, unemployed black man, Hoke, whom Miss Daisy immediately regards with disdain and who, in turn, is not impressed with his employer\'s patronizing tone and, he believes, her latent prejudice. But, in a series of absorbing scenes spanning twenty-five years, the two, despite their mutual differences, grow ever closer to, and more dependent on, each other, until, eventually, they become almost a couple. Slowly and steadily the dignified, good-natured Hoke breaks down the stern defenses of the ornery old lady, as she teaches him to read and write and, in a gesture of good will and shared concern, invites him to join her at a banquet in honor of Martin Luther King, Jr. As the play ends Hoke has a final visit with Miss Daisy, now ninety-seven and confined to a nursing home, and while it is evident that a vestige of her fierce independence and sense of position still remain, it is also movingly clear that they have both come to realize they have more in common than they ever believed <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16748364517049594765">James Goldman was an American Academy Award-winning screenwriter and playwright, and the brother of screenwriter and novelist <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12521.William_Goldman' title='William Goldman' rel='nofollow noopener'>William Goldman</a>.<br/><br/>He was born in Chicago, Illinois and grew up primarily in Highland Park, Illinois, a Chicago suburb. He is most noted as the author of <i>The Lion in Winter</i> and author of the book for the stage musical <i>Follies</i>.<br/><br/>Goldman died from a heart attack in New York City, </span>
  <span id="freeText16748364517049594765" style="display:none">James Goldman was an American Academy Award-winning screenwriter and playwright, and the brother of screenwriter and novelist <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/12521.William_Goldman" title="William Goldman" rel="nofollow noopener">William Goldman</a>.<br /><br />He was born in Chicago, Illinois and grew up primarily in Highland Park, Illinois, a Chicago suburb. He is most noted as the author of <i>The Lion in Winter</i> and author of the book for the stage musical <i>Follies</i>.<br /><br />Goldman died from a heart attack in New York City, where he had lived for many years.</span>
